<hr/></td>
</tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">
Other groups of rights</th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_assembly" title="Freedom of assembly">Assembly</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_association" title="Freedom of association">Association</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_of_asylum" title="Right of asylum">Asylum</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">Civil liberties</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Digital_rights" title="Digital rights">Digital</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_education" title="Right to education">Education</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_a_fair_trial" title="Right to a fair trial">Fair trial</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_food" title="Right to food">Food</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Free_migration" title="Free migration">Free migration</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_health" title="Right to health">Health</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_housing" title="Right to housing">Housing</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Linguistic_rights" title="Linguistic rights">Linguistic</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_movement" title="Freedom of movement">Movement</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">Property</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">Reproductive</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_rest_and_leisure" title="Right to rest and leisure">Rest and leisure</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_of_self-defense" title="Right of self-defense">Self defense</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">Self-determination of people</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">Speech</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Right_to_sexuality" title="Right to sexuality">Sexuality</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Human_right_to_water_and_sanitation" title="Human right to water and sanitation">Water and sanitation</a></li></ul></div>
<hr/></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Rights" title="Template:Rights"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Rights" title="Template talk:Rights"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Rights&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Several theoretical approaches have been advanced to explain how and why human rights become part of social expectations.
</p><p>One of the oldest Western philosophies on human rights is that they are a product of a <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>, stemming from different philosophical or religious grounds.
</p><p>Other theories hold that human rights codify moral behavior which is a human social product developed by a process of biological and social evolution (associated with <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>). Human rights are also described as a sociological pattern of rule setting (as in the sociological theory of law and the work of <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Weber</a>). These approaches include the notion that individuals in a society accept rules from legitimate authority in exchange for security and economic advantage (as in <a href="/wiki/John_Rawls" title="John Rawls">Rawls</a>) – a social contract.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_rights">Natural rights</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">Natural law</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_rights" title="Natural rights">Natural rights</a></div>
<p>Natural law theories base human rights on a "natural" moral, religious or even biological order which is independent of transitory human laws or traditions.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> and his philosophic heirs, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, posited the existence of <a href="/wiki/Natural_justice" title="Natural justice">natural justice</a> or natural right (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">dikaion physikon</i></span>, <i>δικαιον φυσικον</i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ius naturale</i></span>). Of these, Aristotle is often said to be the father of natural law,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> although evidence for this is due largely to the interpretations of his work by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of this tradition of <a href="/wiki/Natural_justice" title="Natural justice">natural justice</a> into one of natural law is usually attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_fathers" title="Church fathers">Church fathers</a> sought to incorporate the until then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">pagan</a> concept of natural law into Christianity. Natural law theories have featured greatly in the philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Su%C3%A1rez" title="Francisco Suárez">Francisco Suárez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hooker" title="Richard Hooker">Richard Hooker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_von_Pufendorf" title="Samuel von Pufendorf">Samuel von Pufendorf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>.
</p><p>In the Seventeenth Century <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> founded a <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">contractualist theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">legal positivism</a> on what all men could agree upon: what they sought (happiness) was subject to contention, but a broad consensus could form around what they feared (violent death at the hands of another). The natural law was how a rational human being, seeking to survive and prosper, would act. It was discovered by considering humankind's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_rights" title="Natural rights">natural rights</a>, whereas previously it could be said that natural rights were discovered by considering the natural law. In Hobbes' opinion, the only way natural law could prevail was for men to submit to the commands of the sovereign. In this lay the foundations of the theory of a social contract between the governed and the governor.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> based his philosophy of international law on natural law. He wrote that "even the will of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnipotent" title="Omnipotent">omnipotent</a> being cannot change or abrogate" natural law, which "would maintain its objective validity even if we should assume the impossible, that there is no God or that he does not care for human affairs." (<i>De iure belli ac pacis</i>, Prolegomeni XI). This is the famous argument <i>etiamsi daremus</i> (<i>non-esse Deum</i>), that made natural law no longer dependent on theology.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> incorporated natural law into many of his theories and philosophy, especially in <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i>. Locke turned Hobbes' prescription around, saying that if the ruler went against natural law and failed to protect "life, liberty, and property," people could justifiably overthrow the existing state and create a new one.
</p><p>The Belgian philosopher of law <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_van_Dun" title="Frank van Dun">Frank van Dun</a> is one among those who are elaborating a secular conception<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> of natural law in the liberal tradition. There are also emerging and secular forms of natural law theory that define human rights as derivative of the notion of universal human dignity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "human rights" has replaced the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_rights" title="Natural rights">natural rights</a>" in popularity, because the rights are less and less frequently seen as requiring <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> for their existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_theories_of_human_rights">Other theories of human rights</span></h3>
<p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Finnis" title="John Finnis">John Finnis</a> argues that human rights are justifiable on the grounds of their instrumental value in creating the necessary conditions for human well-being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Interest theories highlight the duty to respect the rights of other individuals on grounds of self-interest:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Human rights law, applied to a State's own citizens serves the interest of states, by, for example, minimizing the risk of violent resistance and protest and by keeping the level of dissatisfaction with the government manageable</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Niraj Nathwani in <i>Rethinking refugee law</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a> theory considers the comparative reproductive advantage of human social behavior based on empathy and <a href="/wiki/Altruism" title="Altruism">altruism</a> in the context of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Concepts_in_human_rights">Concepts in human rights</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Law" title="Human Rights Law">Human Rights Law</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indivisibility_and_categorization_of_rights">Indivisibility and categorization of rights</span></h3>
<p>The most common categorization of human rights is to split them into civil and political rights, and economic, social and cultural rights.
</p><p>Civil and political rights are enshrined in articles 3 to 21 of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights and in the ICCPR. Economic, social and cultural rights are enshrined in articles 22 to 28 of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights and in the ICESCR. The UDHR included both economic, social and cultural rights and civil and political rights because it was based on the principle that the different rights could only successfully exist in combination:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The ideal of free human beings enjoying civil and political freedom and freedom from fear and want can only be achieved if conditions are created whereby everyone may enjoy his civil and political rights, as well as his social, economic and cultural rights</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights and the International Covenant on Economic Social and Cultural Rights, 1966</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>This is held to be true because without civil and political rights the public cannot assert their economic, social and cultural rights. Similarly, without livelihoods and a working society, the public cannot assert or make use of civil or political rights (known as the <i>full belly thesis</i>).
</p><p>Although accepted by the signatories to the UDHR, most of them do not in practice give equal weight to the different types of rights. Western cultures have often given priority to civil and political rights, sometimes at the expense of economic and social rights such as the right to work, to education, health and housing. For example, in the United States there is no <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal access to healthcare</a> free at the point of use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> That is not to say that Western cultures have overlooked these rights entirely (the welfare states that exist in Western Europe are evidence of this). Similarly the ex Soviet bloc countries and Asian countries have tended to give priority to economic, social and cultural rights, but have often failed to provide civil and political rights.
</p><p>Another categorization, offered by <a href="/wiki/Karel_Vasak" title="Karel Vasak">Karel Vasak</a>, is that there are <i><a href="/wiki/Three_generations_of_human_rights" title="Three generations of human rights">three generations of human rights</a></i>: first-generation civil and political rights (right to life and political participation), second-generation economic, social and cultural rights (right to subsistence) and third-generation solidarity rights (right to peace, right to clean environment). Out of these generations, the third generation is the most debated and lacks both legal and political recognition. This categorisation is at odds with the indivisibility of rights, as it implicitly states that some rights can exist without others. Prioritisation of rights for pragmatic reasons is however a widely accepted necessity. Human rights expert <a href="/wiki/Philip_Alston" title="Philip Alston">Philip Alston</a> argues:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If every possible human rights element is deemed to be essential or necessary, then nothing will be treated as though it is truly important.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Philip Alston<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlston2005807_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlston2005807-95">[95]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>He, and others, urge caution with prioritisation of rights:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...the call for prioritizing is not to suggest that any obvious violations of rights can be ignored.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Philip Alston<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlston2005807_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlston2005807-95">[95]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Priorities, where necessary, should adhere to core concepts (such as reasonable attempts at progressive realization) and principles (such as non-discrimination, equality and participation.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Olivia Ball, Paul Gready<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallGready200742_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallGready200742-96">[96]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Some human rights are said to be "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inalienable_rights" title="Inalienable rights">inalienable rights</a>". The term inalienable rights (or unalienable rights) refers to "a set of human rights that are fundamental, are not awarded by human power, and cannot be surrendered".
</p><p>The adherence to the principle of indivisibility by the international community was reaffirmed in 1995:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>All human rights are universal, indivisible and interdependent and related. The international community must treat human rights globally in a fair and equal manner, on the same footing, and with the same emphasis.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Vienna Declaration and Program of Action, World Conference on Human Rights, 1995</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>This statement was again endorsed at the 2005 World Summit in New York (paragraph 121).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universalism_vs_cultural_relativism">Universalism vs cultural relativism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_relativism" title="Cultural relativism">Cultural relativism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moral_relativism" title="Moral relativism">Moral relativism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moral_universalism" title="Moral universalism">Moral universalism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_ethic" title="Universal ethic">Universal ethic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fgm_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Fgm_map.svg/220px-Fgm_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Fgm_map.svg/330px-Fgm_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Fgm_map.svg/440px-Fgm_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fgm_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map: Estimated prevalence of Female Genital Cutting (FGC) in Africa. Data based on uncertain estimates.</div></div></div>
<p>The Universal Declaration of Human Rights enshrines, by definition, rights that apply to all humans equally, whichever geographical location, state, race or culture they belong to.
</p><p>Proponents of cultural relativism suggest that human rights are not all universal, and indeed conflict with some cultures and threaten their survival.
</p><p>Rights which are most often contested with relativistic arguments are the rights of women. For example, <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> occurs in different cultures in Africa, Asia and South America. It is not mandated by any religion, but has become a tradition in many cultures. It is considered a violation of women's and girl's rights by much of the international community, and is outlawed in some countries.
</p><p>Universalism has been described by some as cultural, economic or political imperialism. In particular, the concept of human rights is often claimed to be fundamentally rooted in a politically liberal outlook which, although generally accepted in Europe, Japan or North America, is not necessarily taken as standard elsewhere.
</p><p>For example, in 1981, the Iranian representative to the United Nations, Said Rajaie-Khorassani, articulated the position of his country regarding the UDHR by saying that the UDHR was "a <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> understanding of the <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> tradition", which could not be implemented by Muslims without trespassing the Islamic law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Littman1999_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Littman1999-97">[97]</a></sup> The former Prime Ministers of Singapore, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kuan_Yew" title="Lee Kuan Yew">Lee Kuan Yew</a>, and of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahathir_bin_Mohamad" title="Mahathir bin Mohamad">Mahathir bin Mohamad</a> both claimed in the 1990s that <i>Asian values</i> were significantly different from western values and included a sense of loyalty and foregoing personal freedoms for the sake of social stability and prosperity, and therefore authoritarian government is more appropriate in Asia than democracy. This view is countered by Mahathir's former deputy:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To say that freedom is Western or unAsian is to offend our traditions as well as our forefathers, who gave their lives in the struggle against tyranny and injustices.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Anwar_Ibrahim" title="Anwar Ibrahim">Anwar Ibrahim</a> in his keynote speech to the Asian Press Forum title <i>Media and Society in Asia</i>, 2 December 1994</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Singapore's opposition leader <a href="/wiki/Chee_Soon_Juan" title="Chee Soon Juan">Chee Soon Juan</a> also states that it is racist to assert that Asians do not want human rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallGready200725_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallGready200725-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>An appeal is often made to the fact that influential human rights thinkers, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, have all been Western and indeed that some were involved in the running of Empires themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Relativistic arguments tend to neglect the fact that modern human rights are new to all cultures, dating back no further than the UDHR in 1948. They also do not account for the fact that the UDHR was drafted by people from many different cultures and traditions, including a US Roman Catholic, a Chinese Confucian philosopher, a French Zionist and a representative from the Arab League, amongst others, and drew upon advice from thinkers such as Mahatma Gandhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallGready200734_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallGready200734-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Ignatieff" title="Michael Ignatieff">Michael Ignatieff</a> has argued that cultural relativism is almost exclusively an argument used by those who wield power in cultures which commit human rights abuses, and that those whose human rights are compromised are the powerless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> This reflects the fact that the difficulty in judging universalism versus relativism lies in who is claiming to represent a particular culture.
</p><p>Although the argument between universalism and relativism is far from complete, it is an academic discussion in that all international human rights instruments adhere to the principle that human rights are universally applicable. The <a href="/wiki/2005_World_Summit" title="2005 World Summit">2005 World Summit</a> reaffirmed the international community's adherence to this principle:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The universal nature of human rights and freedoms is beyond question.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>2005 World Summit, paragraph 120</cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universal_jurisdiction_vs_state_sovereignty">Universal jurisdiction vs state sovereignty</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Universal_jurisdiction" title="Universal jurisdiction">Universal jurisdiction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_sovereignty" title="State sovereignty">State sovereignty</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Universal_jurisdiction" title="Universal jurisdiction">Universal jurisdiction</a> is a controversial principle in international law whereby states claim criminal jurisdiction over persons whose alleged crimes were committed outside the boundaries of the prosecuting state, regardless of nationality, country of residence, or any other relation with the prosecuting country. The state backs its claim on the grounds that the crime committed is considered a crime against all, which any state is authorized to punish. The concept of universal jurisdiction is therefore closely linked to the idea that certain international norms are <a href="/wiki/Erga_omnes" title="Erga omnes">erga omnes</a>, or owed to the entire world community, as well as the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jus_cogens" title="Jus cogens">jus cogens</a>. In 1993 <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> passed a <i>law of universal jurisdiction</i> to give its courts jurisdiction over crimes against humanity in other countries, and in 1998 <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a> was arrested in London following an indictment by Spanish judge <a href="/wiki/Baltasar_Garz%C3%B3n" title="Baltasar Garzón">Baltasar Garzón</a> under the universal jurisdiction principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallGready200770_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallGready200770-103">[103]</a></sup> The principle is supported by <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> and other <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_rights_organisations" title="List of human rights organisations">human rights organisations</a> as they believe certain crimes pose a threat to the international community as a whole and the community has a moral duty to act, but others, including <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a>,  argue that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_sovereignty" title="State sovereignty">state sovereignty</a> is paramount, because breaches of rights committed in other countries are outside states' sovereign interest and because states could use the principle for political reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_and_non-state_actors">State and non-state actors</span></h3>
<p>Companies, NGOs, political parties, informal groups, and individuals are known as <i>non-State actors</i>. Non-State actors can also commit human rights abuses, but are not subject to human rights law other than International Humanitarian Law, which applies to individuals.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">Multi-national companies</a> play an increasingly large role in the world, and are responsible for a large number of human rights abuses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Although the legal and moral environment surrounding the actions of governments is reasonably well developed, that surrounding multi-national companies is both controversial and ill-defined. Multi-national companies often view their primary responsibility as being to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shareholders" title="Shareholders">shareholders</a>, not to those affected by their actions. Such companies are often larger than the economies of the states in which they operate, and can wield significant economic and political power. No international treaties exist to specifically cover the behavior of companies with regard to human rights, and national legislation is very variable. <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ziegler" title="Jean Ziegler">Jean Ziegler</a>, Special Rapporteur of the UN <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="Commission on Human Rights">Commission on Human Rights</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_food" title="Right to food">right to food</a> stated in a report in 2003:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the growing power of transnational corporations and their extension of power through privatization, deregulation and the rolling back of the State also mean that it is now time to develop binding legal norms that hold corporations to human rights standards and circumscribe potential abuses of their position of power.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Jean Ziegler<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In August 2003 the Human Rights Commission's Sub-Commission on the Promotion and Protection of Human Rights produced draft <i>Norms on the responsibilities of transnational corporations and other business enterprises with regard to human rights</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> These were considered by the Human Rights Commission in 2004, but have no binding status on corporations and are not monitored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Additionally, the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_10" title="Sustainable Development Goal 10">Sustainable Development Goal 10</a> aims to substantially reduce inequality by 2030 through the promotion of appropriate legislation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights_in_emergency_situations">Human rights in emergency situations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="685" data-file-width="684" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg/220px-Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg/330px-Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg/440px-Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Camp_x-ray_detainees.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extrajudicial detention of captives in <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_detention_camp" title="Guantanamo Bay detention camp">Guantanamo Bay</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Derogation" title="Derogation">Derogation</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">National security</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-terrorism_legislation" title="Anti-terrorism legislation">Anti-terrorism legislation</a></div>
<p>With the exception of non-derogable human rights (international conventions class the right to life, the right to be free from slavery, the right to be free from torture and the right to be free from retroactive application of penal laws as non-derogable<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-resourceII_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-resourceII-110">[110]</a></sup>), the UN recognises that human rights can be limited or even pushed aside during times of national emergency – although:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the emergency must be actual, affect the whole population and the threat must be to the very existence of the nation. The declaration of emergency must also be a last resort and a temporary measure</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>United Nations. <i>The Resource</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-resourceII_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-resourceII-110">[110]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Rights that cannot be derogated for reasons of national security in any circumstances are known as <a href="/wiki/Peremptory_norm" title="Peremptory norm">peremptory norms or <i>jus cogens</i></a>. Such International law obligations are binding on all states and cannot be modified by treaty.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_inflation" title="Human rights inflation">Human rights inflation</a></div>
<p>Critics of the view that human rights are universal argue that human rights are a western concept that "emanate from a European, Judeo-Christian, and/or Enlightenment heritage (typically labeled Western) and cannot be enjoyed by other cultures that don't emulate the conditions and values of 'Western' societies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Right-wing critics of human rights argue that they are "unrealistic and unenforceable norms and inappropriate intrusions on state sovereignty", while left-wing critics of human rights argue that they fail "to achieve–or prevents better approaches to achieving–progressive goals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFLittman,_David_G.2003">Littman, David G. (19 January 2003). <a class="external text" href="http://www.nationalreview.com/comment/comment-littman011903.asp" rel="nofollow">"Human Rights and Human Wrongs"</a>. <q>The principal aim of the 1948 Universal Declaration of Human Rights (UDHR) was to create a framework for a universal code based on mutual consent. The early years of the United Nations were overshadowed by the division between the Western and Communist conceptions of human rights, although neither side called into question the concept of universality. The debate centered on which "rights" – political, economic, and social – were to be included among the Universal Instruments</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Human+Rights+and+Human+Wrongs&amp;rft.date=2003-01-19&amp;rft.au=Littman%2C+David+G.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nationalreview.com%2Fcomment%2Fcomment-littman011903.asp&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHuman+rights"></span> <span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_journal" title="Template:Cite journal">cite journal</a>}}</code>: </span><span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment">Cite journal requires <code class="cs1-code">|journal=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#missing_periodical" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFInternational_Center_for_Research_on_Women">International Center for Research on Women. <a class="external text" href="https://www.icrw.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/ICRW-WGF-Leveraging-Education-to-End-FGMC-Worldwide-November-2016-FINAL.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Leveraging education to end female gential mutilation/cutting worldwide"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. International Center for Research on Women. p. 3<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">11 May</span> 2022</span>. <q>For women and girls living in areas where FGM/C is prevalent, they are often dependent upon marriage for financial stability. As a result, FGM/C is seen as a way to guarantee a woman’s status, making her able to have children in a socially acceptable way and providing her with economic security, typically provided by the husband. Parents who choose to have their daughters cut consider their decision to be necessary, if not beneficial, for their daughter’s future marriage prospects, in light of the financial and social constraints they may face.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Leveraging+education+to+end+female+gential+mutilation%2Fcutting+worldwide&amp;rft.pages=3&amp;rft.pub=International+Center+for+Research+on+Women&amp;rft.au=International+Center+for+Research+on+Women&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.icrw.org%2Fwp-content%2Fuploads%2F2016%2F12%2FICRW-WGF-Leveraging-Education-to-End-FGMC-Worldwide-November-2016-FINAL.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHuman+rights"></span></span>
